2

N

N
, what we call the final-digits order, an endless dense linear order, which is therefore order-isomorphic and thus also homeomorphic to the rational line 
Q

Specifically, the final-digits order on the natural numbers, pictured in figure 1, is the order induced from the lexical order on the finite binary representations, but considering the digits from right-to-left, giving higher priority in the lexical comparison to the low-value final digits of the number. To be precise, the final-digits order 
n
â
m
 holds, if at the first point of disagreement (from the right) in their binary representation, 
n
 has 
0

m

1
; or if there is no disagreement, because one of them is longer, then the longer number is lower, if the next digit is 
0
, and higher, if it is 
1
 (this is not the same as treating missing initial digits as zero). Thus, the even numbers appear as the left half of the order, since their final digit is 
0
, and the odd numbers as the right half, since their final digit is 
1
, and 
0
 is directly in the middle; indeed, the highly even numbers, whose representations end with a lot of zeros, appear further and further to the left, while the highly odd numbers, which end with many ones, appear further and further to the right. If one does not allow initial 
0
s in the binary representation of numbers, then note that zero is represented in binary by the empty sequence. It is evident that the final-digits order is an endless dense linear order on 
N, just as the corresponding lexical order on finite binary strings is an endless dense linear order.ChatGPTChatGPTThe further elaboration you mentioned from the paper delves into an interesting aspect of the topology being discussed: the final-digits topology on the natural numbers NNN, which is equivalent to the 2-adic metric topology, can also be viewed through the lens of a specific order on NNN. This perspective provides a different yet equally compelling way to understand the structure of this topological space. Let's break down this argument:Final-Digits Order: This is a particular way of ordering the natural numbers based on their binary representation, considering digits from right to left (the least significant to the most significant). This order prioritizes the lower-value final digits in lexical comparison.Ordering Rules:If two numbers nnn and mmm first disagree on a digit when read from right to left, and nnn has a 0 while mmm has a 1 at this digit, then nnn is considered smaller than mmm in this order.If one number is longer than the other (and thus has more leading digits), the longer number is considered:Smaller if the next digit (immediately after the common part) is 0.Larger if the next digit is 1.Structure of the Order:Even numbers end in 0 and thus appear on the left half of this order.Odd numbers end in 1 and appear on the right half.Numbers ending with many zeros (highly even numbers) are further to the left.Numbers ending with many ones (highly odd numbers) are further to the right.Zero, represented by an empty sequence in binary, is in the middle of this order.Endless Dense Linear Order: The final-digits order on NNN creates an endless dense linear order, similar to the lexical order on finite binary strings. This means there are no gaps in the order, and between any two elements, there are other elements of the set.Homeomorphism to the Rational Line QQQ: Since the final-digits order on NNN forms an endless dense linear order, it is order-isomorphic and thus homeomorphic to the rational line QQQ. This is significant because it provides another perspective to demonstrate that the standard model of arithmetic NNN has a continuous presentation on QQQ, as the final-digits order generates a topology that is equivalent to the final-digits topology.This approach of looking at the order properties of the natural numbers and connecting them to topological concepts highlights the deep interplay between different areas of mathematics, such as number theory, topology, and order theory. It enriches the understanding of how seemingly simple structures like the natural numbers can be viewed through various mathematical lenses, revealing complex and intricate relationships.YouThe basic open set

Let me mention the following order, which it seems many people expect to use instead of the final-digits order as we defined it above. With this order, one in effect takes missing initial digits of a number as 
0
, which is of course quite reasonable.

The problem with this order, however, is that the order topology is not actually the final-digits topology. For example, the set of all numbers having final digits 
110

6

Here's a further distilled summary:Continuity of Arithmetic in NNN: The arithmetic operations in the standard model NNN (the natural numbers) are continuous with respect to the final-digits topology. ALI ENAYAT, JOEL DAVID HAMKINS, AND BARTOSZ WCIS LO 
Abstract. Ali Enayat had asked whether there is a model of PA (Peano Arithmetic) that 
can be represented as hQ,â,âi, where â and â are continuous functions on the rationals 
Q. We prove, af i rmatively, that indeed every countable model of PA has such a continuous 
presentation on the rationals. More generally, we investigate the topological spaces that 
arise as such topological models of arithmetic. Finite dimensional Euclidean spaces Rn, 
and compact Hausdorf f spaces do not, and neither does any Suslin line; many other spaces 
do. The status of the space of irrationals remains open. 
1. Introduction 
This paper arose from the following question that asks whether a model of arithmetic 
could be continuously presented on the rational numbers (the motivation for this question is 
explained in Remark 17). 
Main Question 1 (Enayat [Ena09]). Are there continuous functions â and â on the rational 
numbers Q, such that hQ,â,âi is a model of PA ? 
PA (Peano Arithmetic) consists of the i rst order theory PAâof the non-negative parts of 
discretely ordered rings, plus the induction principle for assertions in the i rst order language 
of arithmetic. The natural numbers hN,+,Â·i form what is known as the standard model of 
PA, but there are also many nonstandard models, including continuum many non-isomorphic 
countable models. Although we take PA as a central case, most of our arguments do not 
use the full strength of PA, and our analysis applies to models of various weaker arithmetic 
theories. Consequently throughout the paper, we use the expression âa model of arithmeticâ 
to refer to a model of a suf i ciently strong fragment of PA. The fragments that appear in 
the results of this paper are: Successor Arithmetic (the i rst order theory of hN,0,Si, where 
S(x) = x+1), Presburger Arithmetic (the i rst order theory of hN,+i), PAâ, 
IOpen (PAâplus 
induction axioms for quantif i er free formulas), Iâ0(PAâplus induction axioms for bounded 
formulas), Iâ0+ Exp (where Exp asserts the totality of the exponential function), and IÎ£1 
(PAâplus induction axioms for existential (Î£1) formulas). We refer the reader to [HP98] and 
[Kay91] for general background on arithmetical theories and their model theory. 
We shall answer the main question af i rmatively, and indeed, our main theorem (theorem 5) 
shows that every countable model of Iâ0is continuously presented on Q. We def i ne generally

Which topological spaces support a topological model of arithmetic? 
In section 3, we shall prove that many familiar topological spaces do not support models of 
arithmetic. The list of such spaces includes every i nite dimensional Euclidean space Rn, the 
long line, the Cantor space, and Suslin lines. Meanwhile, there are many other spaces that 
do support topological models, including many uncountable subspaces of the plane R2. It 
remains an open question whether any uncountable Polish space, including the Baire space, 
can support a topological model of arithmetic. 
In light of our answer to the main question in the case of Q, where we showed not only that 
some models of PA are continuously presented on Q, but that all countable models of PA can 
be continuously presented on Qâand the same is true of the indiscrete and discrete spacesâit 
seems natural to ask whether this none/all phenomenon continues with other spaces. 
Question 3. If a topological space X continuously supports a model of PA, then does X 
continuously support a copy of every model of PA of that cardinality? 
We answer question 3 negatively in theorem 30, showing that in every inf i nite cardinality, 
there are spaces that continuously support some, but not all models of arithmetic in that 
cardinality. Thus, the underlying topology is able to distinguish the arithmetic structure. 
One might similarly ask whether it can distinguish the arithmetic theory of the structures. 
Question 4. If a topological space X continuously supports a model of PA, then does it 
continuously support a model of every completion of PA?

theorem (more specif i cally, this follows from the line of reasoning used to prove lemma 20).

Main Theorem 5. Let M be a countable model of the fragment Iâ0of PA. 
(a) M admits a continuous presentation on the rationals Q. 
(b) The ring of integers ZMof M admits a continuous presentation on the rationals Q.

This phenomenon amounts exactly to the continuity of addition and multiplication with

corresponding i nal digits in x and y, and so (x,y) has an open neighborhood in the i nal-digits

If M is a model of arithmetic, and N is a model continuously presented with

The i nal argument of corollary 32 shows the answer is af i rmative when Îº = Ï. More 
generally, the same argument shows that if N is continuous with respect to the initial-segment

shows that when M is Îº-like, then any N presented on the initial-segment topology of M will 
be Îº-like, but it does not quite show for uncountable Îº that the order of M and N agree or 
that these orders are isomorphic. Note that models of arithmetic can have the same order 
without being isomorphic with respect to their addition and multiplication; for example, all 
countable nonstandard models of PA have the same order type: Ï + Z Â· Q. 
5. Topological models of arithmetic in the complex numbers 
In this last section, We provide further instances of topological models of arithmetic by 
revisiting the following result which presents uncountable models of arithmetic in the com- 
plex numbers. The result is due to Josef MlË cek [MlË c73]; see also the second authorâs blog

Theorem 34. Every model of PAâof size at most continuum has a presentation as a topo- 
logical model, using a subspace of the real plane R2. Indeed, every such model is realized as a 
substructure of the complex numbers hC,+,Â·i. 
Proof. Suppose that M is a model of PAâof size at most continuum. Consider the integers 
of this model ZM, from which we may take the fraction i eld and then the algebraic closure, 
building a version of the i eld A of algebraic numbers over M.3So A is an algebraically 
closed i eld of characteristic zero, which has an elementary extension to such a i eld of size 
continuum. Since the theory of algebraically closed i elds of characteristic zero is categorical 
in all uncountable powers, it follows that A is isomorphic to a substructure of the complex 
numbers C, and isomorphic to C itself if M has size continuum. Since M is isomorphic to a 
substructure of ZM, which sits inside A, it follows that M is isomorphic to a substructure of 
C, as claimed. Since complex number arithmetic is continuous with respect to the topology 
of the real plane, we thereby realize M as a topological model of arithmetic using this subset 
of R2.? 
In particular, every countable model of PAâcan be found as a substructure of the complex 
numbers. The same argument applied at higher cardinals shows that if k is the uncountable 
algebraically closed i eld of characteristic zero, then every model of arithmetic M |= PAâof 
size at most the cardinality of k embeds into k. 
Note that by theorem 18, if M is a Îº-like model of arithmetic for some uncountable Îº at 
most the continuum, then no subspace of R2that continuously supports M is analytic.ChatGPTChatGPTIn this section of the paper, the authors delve into deeper and more complex questions about topological models of arithmetic, focusing on the relationship between models with respect to certain topologies and their order types.Question 33: Relationship Between Models and Order TypesQuestion: If MMM is a model of arithmetic, and NNN is a model continuously presented with respect to the initial-segment topology of MMM, do MMM and NNN have the same order type? It must be admitted that the use of geometric intuition 
has no logical necessity in mathematics, and is often left 
out of the formal presentation of results. If one had to 
construct a mathematical brain, one would probably use 
resources more ef i ciently than creating a visual system. 
But the system is there already, it is used to great ad- 
vantage by human mathematicians, and it gives a special 
l avor to human mathematics.Ruelle [1999] 
Higher-dimensional category theory is the study of a zoo of exotic structures: 
operads, n-categories, multicategories, monoidal categories, braided monoidal 
categories, and more.It is intertwined with the study of structures such as 
homotopy algebras (Aâ-categories, Lâ-algebras, Î-spaces, ...), n-stacks, and n- 
vector spaces, and draws it inspiration from areas as diverse as topology, quantum 
algebra, mathematical physics, logic, and theoretical computer science. 
No surprise, then, that the subject has developed chaotically. The rush to- 
wards formalizing certain commonly-imagined concepts has resulted in an ex- 
traordinary mass of ideas, employing diverse techniques from most of the subject 
areas mentioned. What is needed is a transparent, natural, and practical lan- 
guage in which to express these ideas. 
The main aim of this book is to present one. It is the language of generalized 
operads. It is introduced carefully, then used to give simple descriptions of a 
variety of higher categorical structures. 
I hope that by the end, the reader will be convinced that generalized oper- 
ads provide as appropriate a language for higher-dimensional category theory as 
vector spaces do for linear algebra, or sheaves for algebraic geometry. Indeed, 
the reader may also come to share the feeling that generalized operads are as 
applicable and pervasive in mathematics at large as are n-categories, the usual 
focus of higher-dimensional category theorists. 
Here are some of the structures that we will study, presented informally.

rows) f,g,..., 2-cells (arrows between arrows) Î±,Î²,..., 3-cells (arrows between 
arrows between arrows) Î,â,..., and so on, all the way up to n-cells, together with various composition operations. The cells are usually drawn like this:

Here are some key points from the introduction:Role of Geometric Intuition: Leinster acknowledges the importance of geometric intuition in mathematics, as highlighted by Ruelle [1999]. While not logically necessary, visual systems play a significant role in human mathematical understanding and add a unique flavor to the field.Scope of Higher-Dimensional Category Theory: This field involves a diverse array of structures such as operads, n-categories, multicategories, monoidal categories, braided monoidal categories, and more. It is connected to homotopy algebras (like Aâ-categories, Lâ-algebras, Î-spaces), n-stacks, and n-vector spaces, drawing inspiration from various areas including topology, quantum algebra, mathematical physics, logic, and theoretical computer science.Development of the Field: The subject has evolved chaotically with a rush towards formalizing concepts, resulting in a rich assortment of ideas using techniques from a broad range of disciplines.Need for a Unified Language: Leinster emphasizes the need for a transparent, natural, and practical language to express the ideas of higher-dimensional category theory.Main Aim of the Book: The book aims to present generalized operads as a language for higher-dimensional category theory, hoping to make the reader see their applicability and pervasiveness in mathematics, much like n-categories.Informal Presentation of Structures:n-Categories: These consist of cells (0-cells for objects, 1-cells for arrows, etc.) up to n-cells, along with various composition operations. Leinster provides an example of n-categories derived from topological spaces.Multicategories: These have objects and arrows like ordinary categories, but the domain of an arrow is a sequence of objects. This concept generalizes to vector spaces and multilinear maps.Operads: Defined as multicategories with only one object, operads involve sets of 'k-ary operations' and rules for composing these operations. An example given is the operad of linear maps in the context of vector spaces.Leinster's introduction sets the stage for a deep dive into these advanced concepts, making the complex field of higher-dimensional category theory more accessible and providing a foundation for the detailed exploration that follows in his book.YouOperads describe operations that take a i nite sequence of things as input and 
produce a single thing as output. A i nite sequence is a 1-dimensional entity, so 
operads can be used, for example, to describe the operation of composing a (1- 
dimensional) string of arrows in a (1-)category. But if we are interested in higher- 
dimensional structures such as n-categories then we need a more general notion 
of operad, one where the inputs of an operation can form a higher-dimensional 
shapeâa grid, perhaps, or a tree, or a so-called pasting diagram. For each choice 
of âinput typeâ T, there is a class of T-operads. A T-operad consists of a family 
of operations whose inputs are of the specif i ed type, together with a rule for 
composition; for instance, if the input type T is âf i nite sequencesâ then a T- 
operad is an ordinary operad. Fig. 0-A shows typical operations Î¸ in a T-operad, for four dif f erent choices of T. Similarly, there are T-multicategories, where 
the shapes at the domain and codomain of arrows are labelled with the names of 
objects. These are the âgeneralized operadsâ and âgeneralized multicategoriesâ at 
the heart of this book. 
The uniting feature of all these structures is that they are purely algebraic 
in def i nition, yet near-impossible to understand without drawing or visualizing 
pictures. They are inherently geometrical. 
A notorious problem in this subject is the multiplicity of def i nitions of n- 
category. Something like a dozen dif f erent def i nitions have been proposed, and 
there are still very few precise results stating equivalence between any of them. 
This is not quite the scandal it may seem: it is hard to say what âequivalenceâ 
should even mean. Suppose that Professors X and Y each propose a def i nition 
of n-category. To compare their def i nitions, you i nd a way of taking one of Xâs 
n-categories and deriving from it one of Yâs n-categories, and vice-versa, then 
you try to show that doing one process then the other gets you back to where 
you started. It is, however, highly unrealistic to expect that you will get back 
to exactly where you started. For most types of mathematical structure, getting 
back to somewhere isomorphic to your starting point would be a reasonable 
expectation. But for n-categories, as we shall see, this is still unrealistic: the 
canonical notion of equivalence of n-categories is much weaker than isomorphism. 
Finding a precise def i nition of equivalence for a given def i nition of n-category can 
be dif i cult. Indeed, many of the proposed def i nitions of n-category did not come 
with accompanying proposed def i nitions of equivalence, and this gap must be 
almost certainly be i lled before any comparison results can be proved.ChatGPTChatGPTIn this section, Tom Leinster provides a detailed explanation of the role of operads in higher-dimensional category theory, the challenge of defining n-categories, and the importance of visual intuition in understanding these complex structures. Here are the main points:Operads and Higher-Dimensional Structures:Basic Operads: Operads are algebraic structures that describe operations taking a finite sequence of inputs to produce a single output. These sequences are 1-dimensional, suitable for describing operations like composing a string of arrows in a category.Generalized Operads for Higher Dimensions: For higher-dimensional structures like n-categories, more generalized operads are needed. These operads can handle operations where inputs form higher-dimensional shapes like grids, trees, or pasting diagrams.T-Operads: For each type of input shape 'T', there is a class of T-operads. A T-operad consists of operations with inputs of the specified type T and a rule for their composition.Generalized Multicategories:In T-multicategories, the domain and codomain of arrows are shaped and labeled with object names. These represent the "generalized operads" and "generalized multicategories" central to the book.Algebraic Definitions and Geometrical Intuition:These structures, though algebraic in definition, are nearly impossible to understand without visual representations. This highlights the inherently geometrical nature of these concepts.Multiplicity of Definitions for n-Categories:A major challenge in the field is the numerous definitions proposed for n-categories, with little consensus or clear equivalence between them.Comparing Definitions: To compare different definitions, one would typically try to transform an n-category from one definition to another and then back, hoping to end up at the original point or at least at something isomorphic.Unrealistic Expectations: For n-categories, even achieving isomorphism upon this round-trip transformation is considered unrealistic. The canonical notion of equivalence for n-categories is much weaker than isomorphism.Challenges in Defining Equivalence:Defining equivalence for a given definition of n-category is difficult. Many proposed definitions of n-category lack accompanying definitions of equivalence, making it hard to compare or validate them.Leinster's exposition underlines the complexity and the abstract nature of higher-dimensional category theory, emphasizing the necessity of both rigorous algebraic definitions and intuitive geometric representations. The discussion about the multiplicity of definitions and the difficulty in establishing equivalence among them reflects the ongoing challenges and the dynamic nature of research in this field.YouIs this all âjust languageâ? There would be no shame if it were: language can 
have the most profound ef f ect. New language can make new concepts thinkable, 
and make old, apparently obscure, concepts suddenly seem natural and obvious. 
But there is no clear line between mathematical language and ârealâ mathematics.

Here is a summary of the contents. 
Motivation for topologists 
Topology and higher-dimensional category theory are intimately related. The 
diagrams that one cannot help drawing when thinking about higher categorical 
structures can very often be taken literally as pieces of topology. We start with 
an informal discussion of the connections between the two subjects. This includes 
various topological examples of n-categories, and an account of how the world of 
n-categories is a mirror of the world of homotopy groups of spheres.

although, anomalously, operads are best known to homotopy theorists and mul-

Part III: n-Categories

This book is mostly about description: we develop language in which struc- 
tures can be described simply and naturally, accurately ref l ecting their geometric 
reality. In other words, we mostly avoid the convolutions and combinatorial com- 
plexity often associated with higher-dimensional category theory. Where things 
run less smoothly, and in other situations where a lengthy digression threatens 
to disrupt the l ow of the main text, the of f ending material is conf i ned to an 
appendix. As long as a few plausible results are taken on trust, the entire main 
text can be read and understood without looking at any of the appendices. 
A few words on terminology are needed. There is a distinction between âweakâ 
and âstrictâ n-categories, as will soon be explained. For many years only the strict 
ones were considered, and they were known simply as ân-categoriesâ. More re- 
cently it came to be appreciated that weak n-categories are much more abundant 
in nature, and many authors now use ân-categoryâ to mean the weak version. I 
would happily join in, but for the following obstacle: in most parts of this book 
that concern n-categories, both the weak and the strict versions are involved 
and discussed in close proximity. It therefore seemed preferable to be absolutely 
clear and say either âweak n-categoryâ or âstrict n-categoryâ on every occasion. 
The only exceptions are in this Introduction and the Motivation for Topologists, 
where the modern convention is used. 
The word âoperadâ will be used in various senses. The most primitive kind 
of operad is an operad of sets without symmetric group action, and this is our 
starting point (Chapter 2).We hardly ever consider operads equipped with 
symmetric group actions, and when we do we call them âsymmetric operadsâ; see 
p. 34 for a more comprehensive warning.

When n = 0, this is the the unit element.Similarly, an identity arrow in a  category can be regarded as the composite of a zero-length string of arrows placed
